B0023SDPS6|5.0|Craig Johnson provides a glimpse into a particular slice of Wyoming.  He does it with humor, with wit, and with characters who are both realistic and endearing.  I laughed out loud more while reading this novel than I have with any other book in a very long time.  It's not a comedy--far from it--but the characters felt like old friends.  That may say more about my friends than it says about this novel, but if you can appreciate that Wyoming is not the city and that people in small towns don't behave like people in New York City, L.A., or Miami--if you can appreciate that they move at a different and often understated pace--then you could find this novel as delightful as I did.<br /><br />Having said that, I caution readers who love the TV show and who are looking for a novel where the characters look and act just like they did on TV.  I caution folks who love reading about larger-than-life, ultra-perfect characters who get into an extended fight scene in every other chapter.  I caution anyone who is easily offended by language or adult situations.<br /><br />The Cold Dish is not a happy book.  It's not a novel about a main character who's free of faults.<br /><br />It is a mystery novel, but only in the sense that most readers won't know who the killer is until the last chapter.  It is a police procedural, but not one where the main character is consumed entirely by the mystery and thinks not a whit about anything outside that narrow focus.  It's a story about a man in a tough situation who deals with it the best he can but not as well as he thinks he should.  The story is told through a particular viewpoint and that viewpoint has a personality.  If you like that personality, chances are extremely good that you'll like the book--and vice-versa.  I strongly recommend you read the first page or two of the novel before you buy the book.<br /><br />For me, the best parts of this novel are the characters and the depiction of life in small-town Wyoming.  I grew up about three hours from Wyoming and have lived in small towns most of my life.  If you can appreciate these types of characters and this type of setting, I'm not sure how you could not like this book.hree hours from Wyoming and have lived in small towns most of my life.  If you can appreciate these types of characters and this type of setting, I'm not sure how you could not like this book.

B007F0IXD4|5.0|You can find more of my reviews on my co-owned blog Boricuan Bookworms [...]<br /><br />I have to admit that I'm a bit scared after reading this book. I have found a new favorite author.<br /><br />There are few times when a book makes a big impression on you, let it be in any moment in your life. This book is definitely staying with me for a while, I can feel it. A short yet extremely amazing scary story for all ages. You can't be too old or too young to read this masterpiece.<br /><br />While exploring her new home, Coraline meets the weird neighbors with which she shares a big house divided into flats. Everything seems boring and Coraline spends her first days there exploring before school starts. But while in her adventures, she discovers a door in the drawing room that is closed by a brick wall. When she opens it a second time while her parents are away, she is met by a different yet really alike world to her own. She has other parents who claim that will love her forever and always, making each new day an adventure for their loving daughter, but only if she sews two black buttons to her eyes. When she rejects this to her other mother, the real parents are taken from Caroline and the only way to bring them back, and free the mysterious three ghost children, is by challenging the other mother,  who each moment looks less like her mother and something more sinister.<br /><br />I had picked this book in an attempt to not read any ARCs, to read something because I simply wanted and without the obligation of anything, like the old times before a blog and such. A light and interesting read was all I wanted. I wasn't dissapointed. Some people must have seen the 2009 movie of the same name, at least I did before I even knew it was a book. I completely loved it and was even more excited to read the book to be met by such a mysterious and astounding plot.<br /><br />In terms of comparing it to the movie, it actually stuck to be really alike, except for a few things and characters that were probably added for more movie-ish things. The book's  plot didn't fail to keep you constantly guessing, even if you knew some things from the movie. I didn't remember much of it so it helped me to be all suspicious of the other mother's actions. The writing style of Neil Gaiman made it even better, simple yet effective to make your hairs stand up in your arms. It didn't help that I read most of this book at night, like really late at night. There were some things that were simply creepy and disgusting in an enjoyable way because it gave a more realistic feeling to the book.<br /><br />The characters, how do I express myself into telling you how much I loved each one of them? As simple or temporary as they were, each played an important paper in the story, either for the plot as it is the other mother, Coraline, and her parents and ghost kids, or even Coraline's own neighbors (the real ones). They teach Coraline a lesson of life and subconsciously help her through her quest against the other mother.<br /><br />~\\"How do I know you'll keep your word?\\" asked Coraline.<br />\\"I swear it,\\" said the other mother. \\"I swear it on my own mother's grave.\\"<br />\\"Does she have a grave?\\" asked Coraline.<br />\\"Oh yes,\\" said the other mother. \\"I put her in there myself. And when I found her trying to crawl out, I put her back.\\"<br /><br />That's an example of how evil the other mother is. When I read that part I felt the chills running up and down my spine, I got goosebumps, and had to stop reading all together, gasp, and say \\"damn.\\" As evil as she was, she was greatly developed. We don't know what she was in the end, so I suppose it as all part of the mystery of the book to let you thinking what it was. Was it an illusion? A big evil humanoid spider? A witch?<br /><br />My other favorite character was the cat with his sarcastic and sassy remarks towards Coraline. He felt really real and was a great addition to the book.<br /><br />~\\"We... we could be friends, you know,\\" said Coraline.<br />\\"We COULD be rare specimens of an exotic breed of African dancing elephants,\\" said the cat. \\"But we're not. At least,\\" it added cattily, after darting a brief look at Coraline, \\"I'M not.\\"<br /><br />And obviously, I loved to bits Coraline. Stubborn, adventurous, brave, independent, and a fighter, she was amazing for this book. She's exactly how I imagine kids to be, free and curious, yet they can be responsible, sensible, and cunning when needed to be. She was wise beyond her years (even is it's not explicitly mentioned) and it's all expressed in ways for kids to understand, and in ways that stick to your brain.<br /><br />~Coraline knew that when grown-ups told you something wouldn't hurt it almost always did.<br /><br />~Miss Spink and Miss Forcible made a point of telling Coraline how dangerous the well was, and they warned her to be sure she kept away from it. So Coraline set off to explore it, so that she knew where it was, to keep away from it properly.<br /><br />~“Because,' she said, 'when you're scared but you still do it anyway, that's brave.”<br /><br />~\\"I don't want whatever I want. Nobody does. Not really. What kind of fun would it be if I just got everything I ever wanted? Just like that, and it didn't mean anything. What then?\\"<br /><br />A great read you'll probably won't regret, honestly. I was so obsessed with knowing what would happen next, I was even reading between classes and in the classes, even if it was just a page. I was even making my friends crazy showing them interesting quotes or even the creepy and great illustrations by Dave McKean, which is a friend of the author. Those added more to the scary vibe of the book.<br /><br />Another thing I liked was that my version of the book had some bonuses like an author interview, pages from the original writing the author made, and such. With the interview I got to know more about Neil Gaiman and was impressed at how... simple and amazing he sounds, and to know he's a best-selling author of such great books, wow. We get to see more into what's behind the story of Coraline and what is from his life in said story. I particularly liked this he said in the end:<br />\\"It was a story, I learned when people began to read it, that children experienced as an adventure, but which gave adults nightmares. It's the strangest book I've written, it took the longest to write, and it's the book I'm proudest of.\\"<br /><br />Not only has the story inspired me to write, but his words and experience with the creation of Coraline are inspirations for writers of all kinds on that not all great stories are made in a rush or in a year. They can take even more than five years, with the slowest of progressions, and be a memorable piece of writing to stay in the hearts of all who reads it. At least now my mom won't say I never finish writing anything, because Neil Gaiman took quite a while to write this.<br /><br />Now, go buy or ask a friend for this book. Go go go. Don't let much time pass until you sink your teeth into this. Preferably at 12 am for a more spookier feel to it. I guarantee you'll love it, no matter what age you have.<br /><br />(Sorry for such a long review xD)<br /><br />Rating: 5 stars<br /><br />Review by Jennifer Madero :)

B0015346OM|5.0|Black Hawk Down: A Story of Modern War is Mark Bowden’s attempt to explain the events surrounding the 1993, First Battle of Mogadishu. The entire purpose of the book seems to be allowing the survivors of the battle a voice to express what really happened. Bowden writes from the third person perspective of those involved with the conflict – from the elements of Task Force Ranger to the Somalis caught up in the fighting. An extensive array of personalities and backgrounds appear within the pages of the book.<br /><br /> Through recounting the first hand accounts of those who fought and witnessed the battle Bowden exposes the failures that led to the deaths of American servicemen. The entire operation comes across as a mistake and in this Bowden does not come across as biased or jingoistic. The innocent civilians that are caught in the crossfire of the battle are given consideration in Black Hawk Down. If there is a theme to be found within the pages of this piece of journalism it is a warning not to underestimate a technologically inferior foe in combat and to avoid conflicts with no clear solution. The operation is generally considered a failure and Bowden’s writing would appear to confirm this opinion though judging by recent military operations in Afghanistan and Iraq the lessons of Black Hawk Down have not been properly heeded.<br /><br /> Though several books written about the First Battle of Mogadishu exist, Mark Bowden’s Black Hawk Down is widely regarded as the most comprehensive published account. The compilation of so many individual threads makes the book standout. The story is so highly praised that it inspired a film by the same name that also managed to meet critical acclaim.<br /> A broad array of sources was used to write the story. Bowden interviewed dozens of people from both sides of the conflict. As Bowden says, “So many of the men who fought in this battle agreed to tell me their stories that most of the incidents related in this book were described to me by several different soldiers.” This helps to overcome the limitations of memory. The fact that Somalis were interviewed as well as American servicemen speaks of an inherent lack of bias. Bowden also used books and articles as sources during the writing of the book but nothing can compare to the candid stories expressed by those involved in the fighting. Primarily Bowden asks, “What happened?” This is where Black Hawk Down is such a triumph of investigative journalism. There are things about the book that do not paint the United States or the Armed Forces in a positive light. Expressing such things lends credibility to the accounts of the soldiers, civilians, and by proxy – Bowden himself.<br /><br /> Black Hawk Down is extremely well written. Because the story of the First Battle of Mogadishu is told from the perspective of those on the ground (and in the air) it reads more like a novel than a historical text. The prose is gripping and immediate – it is easy to become absorbed in the frenetic pace of the fighting. The little bits of background that Bowden provides about individual soldiers serves to develop an emotional connection, reminding readers that these are indeed real people whose lives were at stake. Because there are no footnotes or annotations the flow of the text is not broken up by peripheral information. It is easy to pick up Black Hawk Down and just start reading it with little to no background of the event. The book is exciting and involving – accessible to intelligent readers and historians alike. Bowden uses military jargon, but does a thorough job of explaining it, and provides background knowledge of the Somalia situation before jumping into the battle proper. It is even worth noting that because of the narrative style of the text, Black Hawk Down can also easily appeal to fans of fiction as well as nonfiction.<br /><br /> In laying it all out there, in telling the soldiers’ story, Bowden succeeds. The themes ofBlack Hawk Down are revealed through narrative rather than soap boxing. This proves to be a far more effective method of persuasive argument than continually harping on something and is without a doubt the greatest strength of the work. All in all, it is possible to set aside Black Hawk Down after reading it and only take away the fear, courage, and ultimately duty felt by the elements of Task Force Ranger. If this is the case then Bowden still accomplishes his primary goal in publishing the story. The book is occasionally frenetic, though never disorganized, and it can be difficult to keep track of the myriad servicemen and civilians involved. These are forgivable sins that are eclipsed by the depth of investigative journalism Bowden puts on display. No one can ever read a single book and expect to know everything there is to know about a subject, but for those interested in the First Battle of Mogadishu, Black Hawk Down is a solid and thrilling place to start.Black Hawk Down are revealed through narrative rather than soap boxing. This proves to be a far more effective method of persuasive argument than continually harping on something and is without a doubt the greatest strength of the work. All in all, it is possible to set aside Black Hawk Down after reading it and only take away the fear, courage, and ultimately duty felt by the elements of Task Force Ranger. If this is the case then Bowden still accomplishes his primary goal in publishing the story. The book is occasionally frenetic, though never disorganized, and it can be difficult to keep track of the myriad servicemen and civilians involved. These are forgivable sins that are eclipsed by the depth of investigative journalism Bowden puts on display. No one can ever read a single book and expect to know everything there is to know about a subject, but for those interested in the First Battle of Mogadishu, Black Hawk Down is a solid and thrilling place to start.

0385743564|5.0|Sanderson takes the maxim \\"absolute power corrupts absolutely\\" and melds it with a story of the mythic heroes of our age, comic book type superheroes.  What would happen if something suddenly started giving normal humans the nearly absolute power of superheroes?  And if the maxim held, then who could stand up to them?<br /><br />The answer, of course, is a David against the Goliaths.  And one Goliath in particular, named Steelheart.  (And, yeah, Sanderson kind of unfortunately gives his David the name: David.  But you get past that.)<br /><br />I'd never read anything by Brandon Sanderson; had never heard of him before.  So I didn't open the book with any preconceived notions.  (I also didn't have any idea it's supposed to be a YA book until after I finished and read something about that - but it didn't have a YA feel to it, certainly not as much as Hunger Games, which I also enjoyed.)<br /><br />I happened to have stumbled on the book from a sci-fi website that mentioned it and had a link to the first couple of chapters.  A smart marketing move, since Sanderson hooked me with his first sentence:  \\"I've seen Steelheart bleed.\\"<br /><br />Sanderson does a great job of imagining what would happen in a world overrun by \\"Epics,\\" his term for the powerful not-superheroes.  It's a place you don't really want to be - the Bizzaro world version of Marvel or DC comics, and not in a good way.<br /><br />The story is about revenge - a staple for the comic genre, of course.  And David joins a group of freedom fighters, or vigilantes depending on your view, called Reckoners.  There are hundreds of Epics loose on the Earth now, and one of the enjoyable things in the book - for me - is Sanderson's invention of several different Epics.  Because he goes into their types, classifications, powers, weaknesses, etc.  He does a good job of world-building for this novel.  I especially liked his brief depiction of \\"the faithful\\" who believe good Epics must arise, and there's a nice description at one point where you get what Sanderson's hinting at, although he doesn't come right out and say it - and for comic book fans, it's a nearly perfect moment in this book.<br /><br />All in all, this is a great yarn.  Lots of fun to read.  Well worth the time and money.<br /><br />I usually read thrillers and mysteries and detective novels.  This was as good as the best of those, with the added element of superheros (or the opposite of them, in this case), which was fun.<br /><br />I'd kinda rather read a stand-alone book than the first of a series.  And though there are some elements that aren't completely explained in this story - such as why people started getting Epic powers - it doesn't detract from this story.  Not sure I'll pick up the next one.  But I don't feel like I need to because \\"Steelheart\\" is a completely satisfying read all by itself.

B00DPH7QFM|4.0|Following Blackmore's departure from Deep Purple in 1993, Ritchie put together a new Rainbow lineup, recorded a fine album (Strangers In Us All) and hit the road. The album didn't sell and within fairly short order Rainbow was folded, with Ritchie opting to form Blackmore's Night with his wife Candice. Blackmore's Night plays medieval renaissance music in traditional acoustic form, which meant Ritchie basically put the Strat into mothballs. Bit of a shame, really, because Black Masquerade finds Blackmore displaying his hard rock technique with a level of ability as strong as it ever was. I'd be disingenuous to claim this was Rainbow's most distinctive lineup: Paul Morris and Greg Smith execute their keyboard and bass parts with competence. Chuck Burgi is technically a fine rock drummer. None of them are likely to be known beyond avid Blackmore fan circles, mostly because they are (as mentioned) competent yet non-distinctive. Doogie White, much like the rest of the band, is technically quite good: as a singer, it's a pretty tall order to essay tunes from such noted vocalists as Ronnie James Dio, David Coverdale and Ian Gillan - along with Graham Bonnet and Joe Lynn Turner - and be able to pull it off. White does, although his voice is almost a bit too flawless and smooth for my tastes if that makes any sense. Not a lot of individual character. The main attraction here is Blackmore, and while I'd agree the bass and keyboards are a bit too low in the mix, for me this was kind of a plus (I mean, are people really buying this on the strength of Paul Morris and/or Greg Smith performing?). It's just good to have what, as another reviewer mentioned, was a fairly well-circulated (in Rainbow fan circles, anyway) show cleaned up and made available for release, as I'd also readily agree with that same reviewer that this release is in far superior condition than any of the bootlegs in circulation. I suppose casual rock fans might say that since this isn't one of the definitive Rainbow lineups the dvd isn't an essential release, and I suppose were I a casual rock fan I'd agree. However, I'm a fairly avid Blackmore fan, and the show was professionally shot, has a good mix, demonstrates Blackmore in excellent form with a highly competent backing group and has a good deal of better known Rainbow and Deep Purple songs alongside the Strangers In Us All material. Well worth it considering it's less than $20.

In interviews Geddy has expressed his disatisfaction with the way Atlantic released their music. I just relistened to the original before purchasing the remix and yep, it is still unlistenable. It is so over-compressed that your ears fatigue after 2 or 3 songs to the point you just have to turn it off. It's a shame too, because there are some really good songs on the album. After getting the remix, I loaded it on my IPod and gave it a listen in the car switching back and forth between the original and the new, and the new mix is head and shoulders above the old (how could it not be), to the point I will just delete the old release. There are actually layered vocals and guitars on this album! Without all the over compression you can hear the layers of guitars come into the mix, as well as the multiple layers of vocals. There is space in the mix to let your ears breath and you can listen to the entire album straight through. I bought the original the week it came out and know the album pretty well, and there are parts that you would never have known were there on the original mix. Little guitar flourishes and vocal harmonies pop now, and I am rediscovering all the songs as if they were new.<br />Some small gripes however are, the vocals too me are a little too out front and there is an odd, small room type reverb on them. Believe me, it is a thousand times better than the old mix where the vocals sounded like they were going through a low-fi filter on every song, but still, they could have been pulled back just a hair. The other thing is the eq on the snare sucks. The one thing the old mix has over this one is the punch of the snare, the new mix has a very dull sounding eq on it, where the old was very snappy.<br />All in all, I am very pleased with the remix version and have been listening to it as if it were all new music. Kudos Rush, BOOOO Warner/Atlantic.

006202406X|4.0|**Spoilers are below**<br /><br />Let me separate my thoughts into two categories: What I felt and What I know.<br /><br />What I felt: That I expected different things to happen. The whole time I was reading, I felt that the explanations that were being given (about genetics, and the reason for Chicago's existence as an experiment, and I guess what you can label as \\"the plot\\") weren't the truth. I expected there to be a twist, something more concrete--a further betrayal, or explanation, what have you.<br /><br />Not to say that Roth's explanations were bad. They weren't. It's her story, not mine. However, the whole time I was reading, I expected something further to take place, and it didn't.<br /><br />When I began the book, the switching of perspectives threw me off a little. I'm not opposed to switching perspectives, though if that's going to happen, I would rather the writer just write in 3rd person. Granted, I feel this is just a personal preference of mine. I'll address it later under the next category.<br /><br />I felt that the plot just kind of did circular things throughout. Like, that it went all over the place only to resolve little? That probably doesn't make sense. I just felt that the main story line was sporadic in its execution.<br /><br />I didn't like the way that Tris chose to reset the compound's memories. I didn't think that was the diplomatic way of handling that problem. While I was reading, I kept thinking that surely she would just destroy the serum, not release it. However, considering Tris' character, I suppose she was just fulfilling her personality.<br /><br />Those were the big things of what I felt. So, moving on.<br /><br />What I know: That I loved the themes of the book, the whole way through. You know, the Divergent series is all about bravery, and what you define yourself as, and if your actions or what other people say about you define you. It's about identity and knowing who you are--the bad parts and the good. And the thing about Allegiant is that it finally told us what the answer is: your identity is found in many things: your choices, your actions, and the love you share with the people around you.<br /><br />At first, Tris' death didn't seem real to me. I was like, \\"Surely they'll find a way to revive her.\\" But they didn't. She really died. And that struck me as the culmination of a lot of things:<br /><br />Tris has done reckless things throughout the whole series. I believe in the principle that if you do enough things that are reckless, they'll catch up to you. And for Tris, it did. But the thing is--her action of going into that room to release the serum and getting shot, it was an act of selfless defiance. A dual Abnegation/Dauntless action. She took Caleb's place; she showed her love for him by choosing to face death herself. And she defied the Bureau, defied the idea that a person's genes determines their identity, their behavior.<br /><br />So, yeah. Tris died, and that was shocking and painful for me to process. But all along, it was what was meant to happen. There's this term called \\"last stand\\", and that was what Tris did. She stood at her weakest, and was still strong enough to show love for her parents, her brother, her friends, and the people of Chicago--who she spent her whole life living alongside. And that is honorable.<br /><br />The last few chapters that are strictly from Tobias' point of view upset me a good bit. It was painful to read about his pain and his loss and how he just wanted to give up, just because I've felt that way before. I empathized with him.<br /><br />But the last bit of the book, where he's talking about how people are broken and there is going to be hurt in the world, but there's hope, because we can be mended, and that we mend each other: that spoke to me at a deep level.<br /><br />Sometimes I think that it's hard for people who have been hurt to let other people in, to trust other people to see their hurt and scars. But without letting people in--without being brave--one can never truly heal. And that was the lesson I got from this book: To let people in, to trust--so I can heal, and maybe they can too.<br /><br />Overall: Allegiant wasn't what I was expecting. But it did teach me a lesson and it spoke to the deeper parts of me. And that's what makes a book truly successful: if it has changed you, or given you a part of yourself back. And Allegiant did that, for me.<br /><br />So thank you, Veronica Roth, for being an amazing storyteller, and for allowing me to read your books. I want to say that they have shaped me into a better person.

006202406X|3.0|I won't bother with plot recaps in this review, but I will share my feelings about this book. I looked forward to it for quite some time, as did many fans of the trilogy. I'd give it three stars for a couple of reasons. I personally think trying to portray the emotion and reaction of people who discover they're all basically lab rats in an experiment is an incredibly challenging subject matter. The disappointing thing for me is how shallow the reactions are from the characters. They seem to nod and absorb the information, or information overload as it were. It's almost a numbness. The airplane ride, the tablets, the computers, the wide, wide world in which they live: it is so nonchalant. There are twists that feel cut off. Sometimes I feel like the book should've been longer, more fleshed out, so we could feel the confusion, the chaos, the shell shock that our characters must have gone through. Instead, they fall right into their roles. It felt rushed. From a generalized standpoint, the plot was believable, but the characters reactions to their surrounding just weren't for me. They grew, the matured, they developed, they forgave, they were human. But, it wasn't the strong finish I had hoped for, and it left me feeling like I wish I'd left off reading after Tris and Four forgave each other. For me the book ended when Tris realized that a mature relationship was one in which you must choose to love every day.<br /><br /> ***THE REMAINDER OF THIS  REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS, SO DON'T READ ON IF YOU DON'T WANT TO KNOW THE ENDING!***<br /><br />When they return from the outside, they're just accepted. It's okay. Peace, love, and happiness, and Marcus slinks quietly away to find absolute power somewhere else. Really? Some things just get glossed over, and I wanted to see and feel and live the reactions. Maybe my heart for this story died with Tris. Maybe I should reread it. But knowing the end, I can't. From a plot and storyline perspective, if I was just reading a synopsis, I like the story and felt it a believable outcome.<br /><br />One of the better parts for me is the off the cuff change of plans that Four employs at the end. He finally trusts himself. He finally grows into a person who can find the end without means that are destructive. And, just as he grows, he loses the one person he loves the most. As he gains the love of his mother, he loses Tris. Evelyn's ominous foreshadowing (you will never be in the new world with my son) comes to bear. I understand the author's point, but the fact that this story ends without a chance at happiness for some of my favorite characters knocks it right off of my re-read list. Kind of like Game of Thrones. As long as I don't open book 5, Arya lives. So I will never read it (until George RR Martin assures me she lives, which he won't!). While I thought the plot was well conceived and a fitting end, it is heart wrenching that Tris never gets to see the peace she enables. But that's life. Stark realism. But, in life, there are many ways to be happy and find happiness and I (as many readers) would've rather seen David die than Tris. In the end, a simple bullet kills her. Maybe in a world so dedicated to peace there wouldn't be a place for Tris. But, in the end, I feel like Four. I'm moving forward, but there's a hole in my heart where an amazing ending should be.

B00BD99JMW|4.0|It is no secret that I love &#34;Divergent&#34;- by the end of the semester of my YA class I am sure that the majority of my classmates (not to mention prof) wanted to pull some Tris-like moves on me for CONSTANTLY pushing it on them. That being said, a lot of that love was unfortunately lost on &#34;Insurgent&#34;, with me feeling that it lost too much of the magic from the previous book- with Tris and Four's relationship constantly balancing precariously over the chasm, Tris refusing to give in any way, shape, or form, and so forth. As such, on the one hand I was beyond excited to read this book (thinking that &#34;Insurgent&#34; merely suffered from second book syndrome and that Veronica would redeem herself), but on the other, I was nervous beyond belief (based upon the previous two books it stood to reason that there would be a lot of conflict, violence, and loss in this)- at times, giving myself a stomach ache when thinking about actually reading it.<br /><br />My biggest worries about this book surrounded its ending because I heard vague whispers that THINGS WENT DOWN- as such, I was convinced before even starting it that I would hate it. However, surprisingly, I did not hate the ending, it wasn't near as hard to accept as I had thought that it would be (Plus, I give mad props to Veronica for doing what she did- it's something that I have literally never seen another author do before, them always chickening out at the last minute. She's going to get a lot of hate for it, but when you really think about it, it totally fits with the overall scheme of things). Furthermore, the last twenty or so pages of this book were absolutely gorgeous- I don't often cry while reading a book, but there was definitely some tearing up going on as I read them. Based on the ending alone, I would give the book 5 stars.<br /><br />Furthermore, I did like the fact that from book to book you can see massive character growth- growth seen in Tris and Four especially (something that really became clear to me as I (re)read all three books in the course of two days). Also, the vulnerability exhibited by all of the characters made them extremely relatable and helped me to understand why they did what they did- even those characters that I swore that I would hate until the end of my days, for instance, Peter and Caleb. Last, I did like the duo POV in this book (I always love me some male POV), however, I can't help but wish that Tris and Four's voices were a bit more ferreted out, it was, at times, difficult to determine whose head you actually were in, since they sounded so similar.<br /><br />That being said, what I did hate about the book, and what did drag down the star rating, was how often Veronica put us through the ringer- with petty, jealous high school drama, further conflict after conflict between Tris and Four (I mean, didn't we get enough of this in &#34;Insurgent&#34;- we get it, relationships are hard and need work, trust isn't a given, and so forth), and an inundated storyline (you thought that the faction system in the first two books was confusing? It's NOTHING compared to all of the layers of politics and whatnot going on in this book- kitchen sink, anyone?). Furthermore, I was not a fan of the pace of the book, the setting of much of it, and I absolutely loathed the new character additions (with the exception of one). Combined, around the 350 page mark, I was about to give up on the book because I was just so angry with everything going on.<br /><br />All in all, for me personally it's a toss-up between three and four stars. Overall, I'm glad that I pushed through and didn't give up on the book. While there is admittedly a part of me does wish that this book did end a different way, as I mentioned, I do think that it is what was truly needed and I appreciate Veronica for having the balls to do so. It's clear that she is the most Dauntless person around. Furthermore, I really and truly do hope that Veronica does continue to write after this, not becoming one of those authors like Stephenie Meyer or Suzanne Collins who seemingly drops off the face of the planet after their massive success.er this, not becoming one of those authors like Stephenie Meyer or Suzanne Collins who seemingly drops off the face of the planet after their massive success.

B00000IQCA|3.0|A complete hodgepodge of intersecting and interwoven and conflicting storylines make up the lavishly entertaining yet uniformly mediocre `Pret-a-Porter', a film that has charm and pizzazz and yet lacks a lot of everything else.  With an inspired cast that makes the most of their character's bounty of wit and sarcasm, `Pret-a-Porter' feels like Robert Altman lite, capitalizing on his obvious signature construction and yet failing to feel inspired.  It's fun, but shallow, and while it tries to give the façade of something more, it is nothing more than just that; a façade.  In a world obsessed with fashion and reality shows like `Project Runway', `Pret-a-Porter' may find a nice audience in the here and now, but it has about as much substance as those reality shows as well, so if you want to look at pretty clothes and catty people, this will make your day.  An insightful satire, this is not, even though it wants so badly to be.  Forest Whitaker and Kim Basinger are best in show here and deliver hilarious supporting roles that are bound to steal your attention.  If you are new to Altman though, this could be deceiving.

6305428522|4.0|I just had this feeling that `Quiz Show' was going to be a complete bore.  I was holding off watching it for the longest time, and yet settling in to watch it over the past weekend was a complete joy.  Despite running over two hours, the film has such natural flow to it that you can't help but be absorbed in it from scene to scene.<br /><br />Bravo to Paul Attanasio for his exceptional screenplay.<br /><br />The film, taking place in the 60's, tells the true story of a young reporter who uncovers a scandal in `quiz show' television when he becomes aware that the winners are being fed the answers to fix the show and garner higher ratings.  When young Jewish `every man' Herbie Stempel plateaus with the show `Twenty-One', the producers convince him to throw the game and elect the young, good looking and financially stable Charles Can Doren as the new winner.  He skyrockets to fame because of his charm and soon becomes the biggest winner the show ever had.  And then the reporters come and the allegations come and the pressure pushes down on him and the producers to save face.<br /><br />Quickly.<br /><br />Robert Redford does a great job of pacing his film, giving us just enough to keep us hooked and keep the film flowing, and he manages to splice together different plot points and character arcs with an ease and naturalness.  The cast is also exceptional, with Fiennes charming the pants off the audience and John Turturro delivering a remarkably layered performance as the jilted Herbie Stempel.  It appears that he and Scofield were really neck and neck in the Oscar race (Scofield was nominated for BAFTA and Oscar, while Turturro was nominated for the Globe and SAG) and I really wish that Turturro had prevailed, since he really is the best part of the film.<br /><br />With snappy dialog, rich production values and an ensemble who sells every note (Paymer and Azaria are a marvelous pair), `Quiz Show' gets so much right I can't help but love it.

B000002LIX|3.0|For years I've liked the few Jane's Addiction songs that I sometimes hear on the radio.  Not that I normally seek out listening to the radio of course since it's a generic wasteland of playing the same few songs over and over, but when it's on Jane's Addiction's music usually stands out the most in a positive way.<br /><br />However trying to patiently sit through an entire album with Perry Farrell singing nonstop and hitting many of the soaring notes that he really has no business trying to hit because his range isn't acceptable enough, is sort of hard to tolerate after a while.  Don't get me wrong, he can sing appropriately *sometimes* such as on, the classic, \\"Three Days\\".  The way he sings \\"Shadows of the morning light, the shadows of the evening sun, til the shadows and the light were one\\" is a *magnificent* line, and clearly the best moment of the entire Ritual De Lo Habitual album.  It makes me think of the more soaring moments of legendary rock bands from previous generations.<br /><br />Even though technically Jane's Addiction is an alternative rock/metal band, I feel there's more to them than simply that.  They also, I believe at least, possess space rock influences.  Not only that, but it's a unique kind of space rock the likes of which Pink Floyd or Rush never had.  Due to Dave Navarro's guitar playing, Jane's Addiction often reminds me of adventurous outerspace journeys which may be intentional or not- don't really know what others think about that one.  \\"Been Caught Stealing\\" is another memorable song.  Perry's vocals aren't as intolerable on this track, even though they should be because he's not really singing any differently here compared to the first few songs.  I'm also rather fond of the calm and imaginative way \\"Then She Did\\" develops.  This is another really good tune, though 8 minutes is a couple minutes overlong I think.<br /><br />However the first few songs sound like total noise to me.  Not only does Perry not understand when to keep his vocals under control (because he sounds like he's just blurting out vocals whenever he feels like) but the vocal melodies themselves aren't distinctive or interesting enough to keep my attention.  Jane's Addiction is easily a more improved band when they're writing lengthier songs in my opinion.  Perry knows how to either control or utilize his voice on the lengthier songs.  Keep the short songs faraway from him because they scream filler to me.  Also, \\"Of Course\\" is an awesome song.  Again, Perry's vocals work here.  Keep him away from shorter songs, based entirely on my experience with this album.  Maybe he's better on other albums.<br /><br />So basically Dave Navarro has a wonderful guitar tone, Stephen Perkins drumming is really good too, and Perry Farrell *can* be good when he's not over-singing his lines.  When he keeps his vocals under control, which he often does on the more epic/longer tunes throughout Ritual De Lo Habitual (except for \\"Of Course\\" or when the guitar work covers up most of his vocals on \\"Three Days\\") his voice and vocal style is more appropriate then.  All just opinion of course.  Still a very good band with a distinctive style, and a good album.

006202406X|4.0|I finished the book earlier today after hesitating to even purchase because of the negative reviews. I am glad that a decided to find out for myself. This review contains spoilers.<br /><br />This book definitely has some issues.  Tris and Four have such similar internal monologues that sometimes I forgot which character's head i was currently inside.  The plot points in the middle of the story are at times hazy, with four or five revolutions and counter revolutions happening inside and outside the city within maybe two weeks.  And of course, there is not the fairy tale ending for our young lovers that has come to be standard in the genre.<br /><br />However, I think it is important not to forget why YA literature exists, and why it is so important.  YA lit faces themes that mature, difficult, and complicated on purpose.  While reading can be an escape, there are no stories without conflict! Without trial or strife! This helps prepare readers for the time when they will need to draw on the strength of the characters they loved, and can provide comfort to young people dealing with themes that they shouldn't have to deal with. Allegiant, in creating the Genetically Pure and Genetically Damaged labels, essentially is dealing with all -isms together.  Racism, sexism, classism, etc.  Tris, labeled pure, never for a second believes in this dogma, while otherwise goodhearted characters are happy to accept this explanation of their world.  This message matters enough to look past less than perfect writing and an ending  that makes us sad.  Loss is also a part of life, and as Four decides in the powerful scene with Christina, the way we are shaped by the people that love us make love worth it, even if it comes to an end.<br /><br />So maybe, when a child who reads faces an -ism, in the back of their mind will be the idea that genetic superiority is hogwash, that it it our actions and choices that define us, and just maybe they will be brave enough to stand up and say so.

Brandon Stanton is competent as a photographer, but his technical skill isn't particularly noteworthy, he merely documents what he sees. It is his 15-second interviews that really rise above and breathe life into his several years of accumulated work. It is absolutely in these mini-interviews that accompanied every photo that his strength lies, and it really does make the HONY project exceptional. All this to say that I was excited about the publication, but when it arrived I felt almost cheated. The book itself is smaller in size than I expected for a photo-book (I believe about 8-9 inches) and for the most part, the interviews and quotes from the subjects have been replaced by short little quips by the author (some of which were clever than others) or simply a notation of where the photo was taken. Gone was the inspiration, gone was the warmth and absolute humanness of each photo, and what you are left with is simply a photo-book whose photos are often nothing to write home about. I still love the blog, and I've flipped through the entire book, but it probably won't be one that I will open again and again.

Write Insurgent: Make the girl a little depressed and edgy, make her reckless and unstable, add some fights to the romance. B00DU77QP2|2.0|While the premise of this book held potential, for me it was tarnished significantly by the poor execution in construction of the story itself.  I felt there was promise in the concept of shadowhunters, and their war against the dark underworld to which most of humanity is ignorant, and I was very excited to see where Clare would take it.  Unfortunately that's where the excitement ended.<br /><br />From the first chapter the story was packed with more clichés than I thought existed.  Our protagonist Clary is your `typical' sixteen-year-old; chronically self-absorbed, overly concerned with her image, and engaged in a perpetual war-of-wills with her mother.  Despite that she thinks herself unattractive, others all seem to find her beautiful.  To round it off, she is completely oblivious to the attentions of her long-time best friend Simon, who happens to be a sweet and caring guy, and is very much in love with her.<br /><br />Instead, she finds herself draws to the mysterious, arrogant badass whom she witnesses committing murder in a club.  As it turns out said badass is a shadowhunter named Jace, and the murder victim was an evil demon.  From here, Clary is rapidly tumbled into a world of the supernatural when her mother goes missing and Clary learns that she was in fact a shadowhunter in hiding.  Much action follows, with Clary (predictably) falling for the obnoxious Jace in favour of Simon, within roughly a week of their acquaintance.  In addition to the melodrama of her romantic life, Clary discovers that her mother was taken by a big evil shadowhunter named Valentine, who is in search of a powerful shadowhunter artefact, the Mortal Cup, and she along with her shadow hunter buddies, must stop him.<br /><br />WARNING: Plot spoilers<br /><br />From here the story rapidly descends into the realm of \\"Greek Tragedy,\\" when it turns out that Valentine is in fact her father, rendered corrupt by the power of the Cup after using it to perform experiments on his pregnant wife and unborn child.  Eventually Clary's mother managed to figure this out, and took off with the cup, hiding it and her daughter where she thought they couldn't be found.  As if one \\"I am your father\\" moment is not enough, it is later revealed to Jace that Valentine is also his father, which means of course that the two prospective lovers now believe themselves brother and sister.<br /><br />By this point I simply sat frowning at the book, wondering how on earth I was expected to remain interested when all I'd received at this (rather late) stage was predictability, melodrama and repeated clichés in both plot and characters.  Jace in particular was painful, and even as a sixteen year old reading this I found him utterly unattractive.  The supporting characters were not much better; largely stereotypes with very little depth.  I continued through the second book in the hopes that it would improve, but I was once again disappointed and gave up not long after.  Perhaps it was simply a mistake to read this directly after Jacqueline Carey's stunning Kushiel's Legacy series, with its intricate plot, characters, world building and themes, or perhaps I was just never really going to like this book no matter what.  Regardless, despite that I thought the concepts were cool, I just couldn't handle the characters or the story.  If you can ignore these things, I would recommend it as a light read, most suitable for younger teens.

B00AJLHUA8|3.0|A crime novelist, Ellison Oswald (Ethan Hawke) moves into a house where several members of a family were brutally murdered and the youngest daughter disappeared. Ellison, trying to revive his now dead writing career, intends to write a book about the murders and has moved into the house for inspiration. His clueless wife, Tracy (Juliet Rylance) and his 2 children, are unaware of their new homes murderous past. Ellison soon discovers a bunch of home movies in his attic, and when he views them, finds out they are all snuff films, spanning several decades. He enlists the help of a local deputy, and a professor who specializes in the occult, to try to make sense of the grisly crimes and to establish the connections between the murders despite years, decades, separating each crimes.<br /><br />This creepy horror tale is at times shocking and disturbing despite having minimal gore on display. It's all in the atmosphere and the gruesome subject matter. Watching people being murdered in horrific ways via a grainy home movie is chilling and the super 8 movies are very well done. The locations are dark, with minimal lighting, no sounds, sometimes grainy and always spooky. Some of the home movies start out charmingly enough, with the luck-less victims having a great time either fishing, at a picnic, hanging around in the swimming pool etc then the footage cuts to some horrible executions. The tale then takes a supernatural twist when Ellison discovers a creepy looking face watching from the depths of a swimming pool in one of the film clips and also notices a strange occult symbol that keeps re-appearing and is discovered to represent a malevolent and mysterious pagan deity.<br /><br />The film is very atmospheric, and many of the shots are in complete darkness and the main character is always walking around looking fearful and paranoid, which adds to the films tone. However, I do wish he would have just turned on some lights and saved us all the grief. Dude, you are investigating a strange noise and carrying a baseball bat for protection, walking through various dark rooms of your creepy house, would it hurt you to turn on a few light switches? Geesh. Despite a very creepy atmosphere, the film does not always get it right and there are one too many jump scares too many. There is one scene that takes it to an absurd level and inspired some laughter from the audience when I watched this at the theatre. I imagine laughter was not what the director was going for and this just proves that you can over-do it. The film is a bit predictable and how many supernatural themed horror films have you seen that involve a child who can sense the evil and draws pictures of the evil yet the parents remain clueless? The pace is also a bit slow. This movie is more psychological so this is expected, but having one actor carry the whole picture may not have been a good idea. Hawke is good, but we get far too many shots of him being shocked, or worried, or bored, or scared, and although I liked the idea of keeping the villain a mystery and not giving it a whole lot of screen time, I would have preferred more of it, and less of the annoying children that kept re-appearing throughout the film.<br /><br />Despite the negatives, \\"Sinister\\" is a pretty good chiller with good performances, and a lot of tension and atmosphere and although there are a lot of murders being committed, most of it is not shown, just implied or not clear enough to warrant looking away (even so, I have to admit, some of the home movies, as brief as they were, disturbed me to the core). As is typical of today's horror movies, there is a twist ending and the film ends on a high yet disturbing note. B00BD99JMW|2.0|SPOLIER ALERT!!!<br /><br />I posted this review on another forum in response to the never ending comments about the ending:<br /><br />1. There were too many \\"climaxes\\", and unfortunately none of them happened on a couch in a hotel with hundreds of private rooms. Buh Dum Bum!<br />2. The serums: it makes sense on a \\"futuristic/dystopian\\" level to have some kind of biological technology that would be able to alter people's minds/DNA. The flaw in this installment was that VR didn't fully develop the divergent DNA as evolutionary or just resilient to these particular strains. For example: we know that over time we all develop immunity to some antibiotics. If the Chi town people were around 7-8 generations down of this initial genetic shift, some of them would have developed immunity, therefore the Bureau was needed to keep evolving the strain of serum to meet these needs. VR never went into that aspect, only barely, and mostly just to set Tris apart from the rest of the divergents, or GP's. This plot line, IMO, was necessary for us to tie together the whole reason for Janine wanted to get rid of divergents in the first place, especially since she knew about the outside world. Simply not enough about that, but more to it later.<br />3. Evelyn's choice was unrealistic for the Evelyn that VR created, but not as a mother in general. The more realistic shift would have been for Tobias to give her a truth serum first, and then see how she really feels about him. Then give her the choice to take the memory serum once he sees that she really does love him. I have no clue why VR didn't think of that plot device first.<br />4. Uriah's coma is unrealistic in a world where the bio tech is so advanced that they have no means to revive a brain, but they can alter your genes for memory loss (which only targets certain parts of memory: ID, EGO, etc.)<br />5. The USA concept would have been much more developed if there was another installment, perhaps a prequel story of Edith Prior asa girl or maybe her parents.<br />6. The POV. I'm not sure why no one has mentioned this, but it was almost like VR read the Allie Condie trilogy, which IMO is MUCH better than the Divergent trilogy. In Condie's the WHOLE story is in POV with the two main characters, so you get to see the different personalities develop throughout. This is what makes the difference in creating dynamic protagonists vs. static protagonists. Tris was static: she never really went through any catharsis or personal transformation. She remained true to her original self, albeit a much braver and aware version of that self. It was Tobias who changed and became a second protagonist in the third book. Had she really thought this through she would have had both POV from the first book.<br />7. Not to beat a very dead horse here but Tris' death could have played out with her surviving the death serum, but in her weakened state, she falls prey to the memory serum. This would have kept her alive, but with no memory of Tobias. Then we would have been able to see, if after two years pass, she really truly had those qualities of sacrifice and bravery, as we were able to see that the memory serum really didn't change the core aspects of Peter. Then the real tragedy would have been Tobias having to exist near a person who he loves but has no memory of him. We could have seen him trying to befriend her and with his new self awareness, seen if they could have fallen in love again without all the baggage.<br />8. David was a crap villain compared to Marcus, and Marcus just breezed right on out into the fringe. A worse conclusion for David could have been that everyone finds out about who he really was and they make him till fields or some other crappy job. But because he works for the government he gets to still hang around? Once again, if memory serum just erases your memory, but leaves your core personality traits intact, then he would have eventually reverted to his old self.<br />9. Tobias workingfor the new government with Johanna is a great way for him to explore his new found qualities, but how about telling us what plans they have for this new government? What are his job duties? Is he trying to make do with what they have, or actually find a way to make a better world? Once again, this concept is much more fleshed out in Condie's novels.<br />10. Family first? Tris said her family was dead and Tobias was her new family. It is a point in this world that they are able to choose the people who they consider family by picking a new faction or staying with the ones they came from in the first place! Why did she suddenly feel the need to sacrifice for a brother who tried to kill her and never explained his reasons other than his boss was persuasive? puh-leeaaasseeee!<br />11. Since it's a YA novel, I will agree that the scenes were a bit racy, but even in Twilight they consummate after a wedding, so it's something that VR could have addressed with a similar plot device. However, I come from the generation of Judy Blume where teens having sex is a realistic way to shape and develop characters. It seems that this sexual tension between T & T was going to have to come to a conclusion at some point.<br /><br />In conclusion:<br /><br />Disappointing for a final in a trilogy. Some editor, somewhere in NYC, is getting fired for rushing this book, when clearly VR needed more time to think and develop her ideas. You don't see anyone rushing George RR Martin, because we don't mind WAITING FOR QUALITY!!!!!<br /><br />WOW! I think I just wrote a book report!<br /><br />I should have written this sodding trilogy myself!<br /><br />Who's with me???!!!!!

B00BRUQ7ZY|3.0|First, let me say that Aslan is an excellent writer, which shouldn't come as a surprise given his background and education.  This work is no exception.  The book is an easy read and - for non-fiction - is paced very well.  Most folks can read the book in a day or so.<br /><br />The book also provides some interesting context for the story of Jesus insofar as the culture and times in which he lived.  It is not (and does not purport to be) a scholarly work.  Well, I say it doesn't purport to be, but Aslan does claim to have a some scholarly background in theology and religion and does represent the book as having been well if not meticulously researched.  Like some other reviewers, I knock a star off for not footnoting the work. If you're going to include references in each chapter to source material, then connect the source material to the specific claims you make.<br /><br />I knock another star off for over-stating as fact what virtually every scholar on every side of the question of the \\"historical Jesus\\" acknowledges as informed guess-work.  For instance:  we don't \\"know\\" when any of the gospels were written and Aslan consistently points to the latest possible dates for most of the gospels as if these dates were uniformly accepted if not established fact (at least he has them written within a hundred years of the death of Jesus - I saw one comment following another review that stated that the books were written \\"centuries\\" after the event!)  Another instance: he frequently cites to \\"Q\\" (Quelle) as if we actually had the document.  We don't.  Aslan does ultimately admit this, but a casual reader may very well conclude the document actually existed when in reality no one can make that claim (although many scholars consider it \\"likely\\").  One other instance - his characterization of the ending of Mark (the ending most scholars do agree upon - Mark 16:8).  I would simply encourage readers to take a look at the first eight verses of that chapter and compare it to what Aslan says about it to see for themselves what I'm saying.<br /><br />I won't knock a star off for bias because, although it is biased, it is clearly meant to be.  Aslan has a premise, clearly stated from the outset (it's in the title for goodness' sake): Jesus was a zealot like preacher who others subsequently overlaid with a message of peace.  This is not the consensus of New Testament or First Century scholars, however.  Aslan, in support of his premise, gives little acknowledgement of this.  Again, I don't knock off a star for this because Aslan is up front in telling us that these are his conclusions.  He's obviously read many others works and has developed his own understanding of where they lead.  Knowing that up front, I have no problem in recommending you read the book with the caveats already mentioned.<br /><br />If you do read the book, then I'd encourage you to read some of the works cited by Aslan in order to draw your own conclusions.  In particular, I'd recommend reading the more accessible works of N.T. (\\"Tom\\") Wright, as he is also eminently \\"readable\\" (except for his scholarly works).  I'd also recommend \\"The Writings of the New Testament\\" by Luke Timothy Johnson for another perspective on the Judaic, Greco-Roman, Hellenistic and Mediterranean culture of the time.<br /><br />One thing that Aslan (as well as other scholars) seem to miss is: \\"why?\\"<br /><br />Why did the writers of the four canonical gospels (not to mention the gnostic gospels) write what they did?  Why did these writings have the impact they did from so early on?  Some might argue that Aslan does address this within his major premise: that the evangelists wrote to conform the historical Jesus to the post-temple, Roman dominated and Greek influenced world.  I would first suggest that this is a simplistic explanation for what happened.  But I would also suggest that it misses the first and fundamental question of why there was a need to do so in the first instance.<br /><br />For the answer to that seminal question look elsewhere.  In the meantime, if you want to venture into the general area of the \\"historical Jesus\\" then Aslan's work, despite its shortcomings, is eminently readable and may whet your appetite for more.e answer to that seminal question look elsewhere.  In the meantime, if you want to venture into the general area of the \\"historical Jesus\\" then Aslan's work, despite its shortcomings, is eminently readable and may whet your appetite for more.

He also discovers the secret of the Horror House ride...it's haunted.<br /><br />The story goes that Linda Gray came to the fair with her boyfriend. The boyfriend takes her on the Horror House ride and halfway through, slits her throat and tosses her out. All he leaves behind is a bloodied shirt and a pair of gloves. Now it is believed that Linda Gray haunts the ride waiting for her killer to be found.<br /><br />This story intrigues Devin and he decides to dig deeper to see if he can discover the identity of the killer. This fascination leads Devin on an interesting adventure that ends up putting him and his new found friends in danger.<br /><br />King has a way of making two complete different story lines come together flawlessly in the end. At times while you are reading you start to wonder what the importance of a scene is, but as long as you put your trust in King, he won't let you down. 006202406X|2.0|I considered giving this book one star, but, seeing as I was able to read it through, I can't deem it as awful.<br /><br />I'm an avid reader of fiction, with a particular interest in dystopian novels (The Giver was my first experience with this genre in elementary school). I've experienced the unparalleled writing styles of everyone from King to Collins to Rowling, and I began to hold Roth to the same standards.<br /><br />Divergent and Insurgent were captivating from start to finish, with Insurgent ending with an odd, but somewhat believable plot twist. Allegiant, however, saw the breakdown of what could have been a potentially multi-dimensional plot into one marked by shallow climaxes and repetition. The sudden change into a POV storyline seemed an interesting twist at first, but it was hard to distinguish Tris from Tobias. I thought (several times) that I would have to flip to the beginning of the chapter to remember whose POV I was in, but the other \\"lover's\\" name would often show up within the next line or two. I'm all about romance in a novel, especially since \\"Love & Romance\\" is a category the trilogy falls under, but the kiss scene often bordered on superfluous and even wasteful. I'd grown used to the action of the first two books, so to have that replaced with mini-fallout missions and intimate scenes seemed an unfair tradeoff.<br /><br />The character development was...lacking...to say the least. I read a review that saw Tobias as a character who began to think backwards, and I couldn't agree more. Rather than making Four reach his full potential, he seemed to retrogress into a more immature state until finally becoming a mere shadow of his former self by the end of the novel. Now, I understand that dealing with the loss of a loved one means losing yourself in grief, but it seemed that, after a while, his narration felt more like shallow a stream of consciousness rather than an intense narrative that should be given by someone of his age (although there were a few movements where his maturity did appear). As far as other characters are concerned, there were far too many that were underdeveloped. David, particularly, had the potential to become someone sinister with a more obvious \\"plot twist\\" of his love for Tris's mother (which was apparent very early on). At one point, there were just many other characters that only did a couple of things each; I felt like many of the attributes could have been given to one or two new people, who could have in turn been dynamic characters, rather than just many static ones.<br /><br />The most disappointing element here was the senseless killing off of characters. I can understand the deaths of some major characters as necessary to move a plot along (Potter series) or to illustrate the inevitability of saving someone close to you (Hunger Games trilogy), but it is an entirely different thing when the protagonist is killed (by a character who honestly didn't deserve the right--and got away with it later) with no useful plot movement. Tobias had already neutralized the problem back in Chicago, and Tris had done the same back at the Bureau. An \\"ultimate sacrifice\\" of her somehow coming in contact with the memory serum and Tobias having to deal with the repercussions of it would have been a more reasonable ending that wouldn't cause such a disloyalty between the Roth and her fans. The fact that Four could hear Tris' voice calling him through the haze of the attack simulation (with no other explanation but \\"the power of love\\"), but Tris could not be saved from death (even after finding out she was immune to the death serum) seemed like an even greater insult; the logic was simply discontinuous.

B00BD99JMW|4.0|First and foremost, this is a YA novel. It is not Orwell or Huxley, so expecting the writing to fly off the page and create a vivid, disturbing world is completely unrealistic. That said, the writing was oftentimes slightly disappointing when compared with the other novels.<br /><br />Getting immersed in the novel is troublesome: it is halting and stuttering for the first half. Roth seems to have lost her rhythm and keeps throwing the exact same obstacle at her characters disguised as different problems (A rebellion here... a rebellion here... a rebellion here... a rebellion here... it is an interesting look into a country that's collapsing, but it becomes quite too predictable).<br /><br />As interesting as it was stylistically to switch chapters between Tris and Tobias's point of views, that creates a disjoint: there is none of the continuity of the first two books. Once you're immersed in Tris's narrative, her chapter ends, and suddenly you are forced to remove yourself from her brain and try to enter Tobias's head. Also, the style of voice for each character isn't quite differentiated enough to really allow this separation to fully take place. There were times when I would forget whose head I was in, and it was simply because the style did not change enough to move me from one to another.<br /><br />However, having finished the book, the conceit makes more sense. We had to have a look into Tobias's head.<br /><br />As for those who disparage Roth for ending it the way she did, I, too, was disappointed. Yet Tris always did things true to her character: things she had done in the first book and in the second. There was no other way for Roth to end the series and have Tris remain an authentic person. Sure, we all want a happy ending, but doing so would be a cop out. How likely would it be for a sixteen year old girl to survive all of the things Tris has survived? Not very. Add that to her self-sacrificing tendencies and what you get is exactly what Roth wrote. WasI angry? Yes. Heartbroken? Definitely? Did I spend the last eighty pages or so of the book crying, wiping my eyes, aware of what was going to happen? Yeah. But then again, I'm a writer, and as a writer, I would have been extremely frustrated if Roth had ended it happily for everyone. That's simply not realistic.<br /><br />So while I am sad that I didn't get the storybook ending a la The Hunger Games or Harry Potter, I am happy that, for once, an author was realistic about what would happen to a traumatized sixteen year old girl in extraordinary circumstances who has survived way longer than probable.
B000003NA4|4.0|Diana Krall was one of the first female Jazz singers that I collected. I have 7 CDs of hers & this is my favorite. Maybe because it was my first CD of hers I bought & tend to judge music from the original source. I read some of the negative reviews & I am shocked. Some write that she can't sing; that she is too airy or forcing a female version of Frank Sinatra. Sure there are better singers out there in the Jazz world, I prefer Cassandra Wilson, but Diana Krall has a certain emotional match to the words. She does more than sing, her voice expresses the meaning. Some write that there are better female piano players out there & I agree: Patricia Barber is one of the best jazz piano players but her music is less romantic & sometimes the songs have weird subject matters. Therefore Diana Krall is a package deal: a fine singer & piano player with a certain style. This is a great CD both in song & sound production. I give the CD a rating of 4 because my tastes lean towards the experimental & this CD is almost on the POP side. To most popular Jazz lovers this is surely a 5. If you're just starting your Jazz collection, this would be a good place to start.

B00A6CCF0K|4.0|When I heard that Stephen King was writing a sequel to what I consider one of his best novels The Shining, I was both eager and hesitant to pick it up. Part of me was eager to see where King would take the characters from the world of the Overlook Hotel in the sequel and hesitant because of the track record of other authors with \\"long awaited\\" sequels.<br /><br />The good news is that Doctor Sleep is a worthy and successful sequel to The Shining on just about every level. And I think it's a good thing that King wrote it thirty or so years after the original hit our shelves.<br /><br />Picking up right where The Shining left off, Doctor Sleep chronicles Dan's attempts to not become his father's son. Dan reaches rock bottom when he steals the last $70 from his single-mother, one-night-stand's purse, after blowing his entire paycheck on drugs and booze the night before. The incident haunts Dan throughout the novel and serves as a motivation to change and help others. He does this working in hospices, using his power to see dying people through their final moments of life and when he becomes aware of another young person who shares his unique ability.<br /><br />That is Abra, a young girl who may be even stronger in her power than Danny was and is. Her abilities confound her parents as well as making her a bit of an outcast. In addition, it brings her onto the radar of a nomadic group of people who feed on \\"the steam\\" of young people with psychic abilities. To the group, Abra is like a buffet of extended-life and health-benefits for them and they're eager to find her to not only feed now, but to stock up for a long time to come.<br /><br />King spends the first half of the novel putting introducing the characters and plot threads before he begins to pull out all the stops in the second half. The novel feels like a train, building up speed slowly before racing to its ultimate, page-turning finish. And yet at over five-hundred pages, Doctor Sleep never feels like its overstaying its welcome or treading water. In fact, had it gone another hundred or so pages, I would have been perfectly content to keep on reading and spending time with these characters.<br /><br />It's a testament to King's second-half career renaissance that he can pull off not only a book this good, but a sequel that works this well. He may have taken close to thirty year to write the novel, but I'm glad he had that time to hone his craft and to really decide if a sequel was necessary and would add to his original novel.<br /><br />King also manages to throw in a bit of a shout-out to his son Joe Hill's latest novel NOS4A2 in the early goings of the novel. And while I didn't catch a shout-out to his daugher-in-law Kelly Braffet's brilliant novel, Save Yourself (review coming soon but do yourself a favor and pick up a copy immediately), I wouldn't be shocked it there was one in there as well. It even contains a bit of a homage to King's recent Hardcase Crime entry Joyland.<br /><br />Of course, the big question that most fans will be wondering is -- is this novel a worthy sequel to The Shining?<br /><br />The short answer is, yes, absolutely.<br /><br />The Shining was the story of one man's alcohol-fueled descent into madness. Doctor Sleep is (partially) the story of the impact those events had one on man and how he deals with it over the course of the next thirty years. Dan Torrance, for good and bad, is his father's son. And while Dan doesn't do something quite as viscerally repulsive as Jack breaking young Danny's arm in a fit of rage, his low point of taking the cash and his internal justification for doing so show him to be just the kind of guy his dad was. Dan's struggle his addictions and his power throughout his life inform a large portion of the novel and the choices made. It's a fascinating character study by King and, in many ways, that sets this novel at or near the same level as The Shining.<br /><br />But Doctor Sleep is also competing with my fond memories of reading the original and being absolutely glued to every page. It's hard to recall that much of The Shining is a slow burn, building up to the pulse-pounding climax. It's easy to remember the horrific nature of Jack Torrance's chasing his wife and son through the hotel and not recall everything King did to build up to that point.<br /><br />Doctor Sleep starts on a slow burn as well and builds to a pulse-pounding finale. But the seeds King sews about his characters make the first half just as memorable as the second -- at least in my short term memory. It will be interesting to see how in the months and years to come what I remember from this novel as compared to the original.<br /><br />In the end, Doctor Sleep is a worthy successor to The Shining and another triumph for Stephen King. If you haven't read the original, I can't recommend it enough. And then give this one a try. It's a very different reading experience but one that is just as satisfying.mories of reading the original and being absolutely glued to every page. It's hard to recall that much of The Shining is a slow burn, building up to the pulse-pounding climax. It's easy to remember the horrific nature of Jack Torrance's chasing his wife and son through the hotel and not recall everything King did to build up to that point.<br /><br />Doctor Sleep starts on a slow burn as well and builds to a pulse-pounding finale. But the seeds King sews about his characters make the first half just as memorable as the second -- at least in my short term memory. It will be interesting to see how in the months and years to come what I remember from this novel as compared to the original.<br /><br />In the end, Doctor Sleep is a worthy successor to The Shining and another triumph for Stephen King. If you haven't read the original, I can't recommend it enough. And then give this one a try. It's a very different reading experience but one that is just as satisfying.

That included devouring the books and creating their own fan-fic based set within the world of Simon.<br /><br />Now that two are headed off to the same college and Cath is having a difficult time adjusting to the changes in her life. The story is populated with a variety of authentic, likeable characters for Cath to interact with, including her roommate. The story of Cath's struggles is one that can and will appeal to many, regardless of your age.<br /><br />One of my favorite books I've read this year.
