B00BH0VSM6|4.0|THE BAT is the first in the series, but was only recently made available in English and for Kindle. I was thrilled to read it, as the subsequent books in the series refer to Harry's investigation of a serial killer in Australia. But as I gobbled it up, literarily speaking, one thought kept surfacing: this is a strange way to start a detective series.<br /><br />The book introduces Harry Hole as an Oslo detective sent to Sydney, Australia to assist in the investigation of the murder of a Norwegian woman who was a moderately successful Norwegian TV star. His guide throughout the investigation is an Australian detective named Kensington who is of Aboriginal descent. Kensington's boss isn't thrilled to have Harry there and wants to shut him out even as Kensington keeps introducing Harry to strange folks in the outback as well as in bars in Sydney's red light district called King's Cross.<br /><br />Now, I've been to both Oslo and Sydney (including a night of clubbing in King's Cross) and the two cities have a lot in common. They are both vibrant and modern with an athletic vibe and a well-educated populace.  Lots of tall white people in rock band tees. Just like Harry.<br /><br />But Nesbo makes the differences really speak to the reader by using the murder investigation to reveal the lifestyle, history, and integration difficulties of Australia's Aboriginal population. We discover pain and passion through Harry's eyes in a way that neither the Norwegian detective nor the reader expect to do so.<br /><br />The serial killer does bad stuff, the ending is full of suspense, a romance goes awry, and the roots of Harry's self-destructive behavior--more of a central issues in later books--are revealed. But overall, I can't shake the feeling that this was an odd way to start a mystery series, because at no time do we see Harry as particularly Norwegian or in his natural element. We don't meet his colleagues or understand the context for any continuing series. I recognized places in Australia, and loved the great descriptions, easy dialogue, and twisty plotting. But I'm not sure I'd be compelled to read more in a series set in Norway if I only had this story to go on.<br /><br />Bottom line? Read THE BAT by Jo Nesbo, but not as your introduction to the Harry Hole series.
B00EKQ3YZI|4.0|The new remix album, \\"Love to Love You Donna\\" contains 13 cuts, of which one is a 'new' song that was recorded shortly before Donna Summer's death in 2012.  For the most part, the album is enjoyable, though none of the remixes outshine the original recordings.  As with any remix album, there are highs and lows.  First, the good:<br /><br />Giorgio Moroder's reworking of \\"Love to Love You, Baby\\" amps up the bass and the tempo and sprinkles in a few musical samples from his 70's work including \\"I Feel Love\\" and \\"Utopia.\\"  While this production doesn't veer too far from the original, it is a fun listen.<br /><br />Frankie Knuckles and Eric Kupper tackle \\"Hot Stuff\\", stripping the song of its original 'rock' element and turning the sultry 'come on' into an airy, late-night groove.  Totally listenable, though the sexual tension and immediacy of the original is lost in the remix.<br /><br />\\"Bad Girls,\\" as remixed by Gigamesh, retains the playfulness of the original recording, as well as most of the vocals.  It's a fun, bouncy reinvention that is sure to fill a dance floor or two.<br /><br />The Masters at Work remix of the seminal \\"Last Dance\\" begins awkwardly (the vocals don't match the beat), but eventually synchs up and manages to build to a powerful crescendo about three-fourths through the cut.  Give this one a chance and you will play it often.<br /><br />Both the Afrojack and Benga remixes of the electronic classic \\"I Feel Love\\" are pleasant but don't really add anything we haven't heard before in every remix of the song that has been released since Patrick Cowley's version in the early 80's.  I'm not sure anyone could improve on the original.<br /><br />And finally, \\"Working the Midnight Shift,\\" as imagined by Holy Ghost, is the absolute best cut on the album, with a very current backing track and a vocal by Tiffany Roth.  This production would sound right at home on any urban radio station today.  While it would have been nice to have Donna Summer's original vocals, I'm assuming they simply were not available in a format that allowed for a clean-sounding, modern production.  In addition to the stellar backing track, the song works well since it was never a hit single and is not competing with the memory of Top 40 days gone by.<br /><br />And now for the rest:<br /><br />Both Duke Dumont's \\"Dim All the Lights\\" and Jacques Greene's \\"On the Radio\\" are curious mis-steps that retain none of the fun of the original recordings (or much of the vocals or melody).  For completists only.<br /><br />Chromeo's take on \\"Love is in Control\\" is competent and current, but the original song wasn't very good to begin with so the ridiculous lyrics are made even moreso in this stripped down version.  The original song  made the US pop chart based solely on the reputation of the participants (it was produced by then super-hot Quincy Jones) and fell off the Top 40 chart like a ton of bricks after peaking at #10.<br /><br />Laidback Luke's remix of \\"MacArthur Park\\" is pure cheese and really illustrates that the best way to present a dance version of this complex song is exactly how it was done by Giorgio Moroder and Pete Bellotte in 1978.  Painful to the ears in either the dub or full vocal versions.<br /><br />I love Hot Chip, but their version of \\"Sunset People\\" slows down the tempo of the original to the point that it is boring.  They have managed to turn one of Donna's most frenetic and exciting songs into a mind-numbing, lethargic mess despite the fact that they had the original vocal track and musical stems from which to build a new song.  Yikes.<br /><br />And finally, the 'new' song \\"La Dolce Vita.\\"  I really was hoping that this tune was going to be on par with Donna's classics, but the best thing about it is the hooky refrain.  The production of the backing track is quite good, but the lyrics of the verses are repetitive and simple-minded, which ultimately sink the track.  It is, however, nostalgic to hear Moroder on the vocoder and serves as okay background music.<br /><br />Many fans are complaining that the remixes don't do justice to the legacy of Donna Summer.  I'm guessing the complainers haven't played the best cuts loud on a home stereo or are so stuck in the 70's that they can't accept that these remixes were really made to capture a new, younger audience.  They definitely sound amazing at full volume and are sure to get you up and dancing if you give them a chance.

For the first hour or so, it is a psychological thriller (the stand-off between the Japanese camp leader and the British officer, played brilliantly by Alec Guiness. When the British officer protests yet again that something is against \\"the rules\\", the Japanese officer hisses back \\"this is war! this is not a game of cricket\\", just fabulous. After that, we see the slow evolution into a nail-baiter, even though we all know how it's going to end. It's hard to imagine how a movie like that could be made today. Bottom line: 55 years on, this movie stands the test of time, and easily rewards repeat viewing.<br /><br />As to the Blu-ray: it looks gorgeous, but the upgrade is not as mind-boggling as was \\"Lawrence of Arabia\\". But that is perhaps not a fair comparison. All that aside, if you are \\"old school\\", you will love this again. For the younger generation: by all means, check this out, I think you will be more than pleasantly surprised. HIGLY RECOMMENDED!

There were points that were tedious, there were elements that were discussed in far too much detail to be interesting, and there was very little further character development.<br /><br />I hear the good reviews, but most of them don't state why it was good, so I'll try my best.<br /><br />So here's how I feel.<br /><br />Yes, there were several lacking points of the book. And Tris- I don't know that she ever does. The rest of the characters never change, and it is disappointing to watch. There are also elements brought in that make the book scientific and dragging at points. You as the reader are so concentrated on figuring out what Roth is saying that it can be difficult to stay invested in the story. For the new characters introduced- they are one sided, single minded people with the purpose of advancing the plot.<br /><br />I'm still trying to define the true reasoning for the end of the book being the way it was. Was it predictable? Probably. Was it alienating? A little. Were there better ways to get the point across? Most definitely. But the thing is that the important part was the message. More than anything, Roth wanted her readers to know that love is the answer. It is good to be brave, and the bravest thing you can do is to love- love with your entire self and never feel the need to hesitate with your heart. Also, look who she's writing for. Young adults need the message to stop fearing love, to open themselves up to their emotions, to find that bravery does not mean sacrificing yourself or the parts of you that people think are wrong. And she got the message across. It may have been a little cheesy and overdone, but it may have been exactly what her target audience needed.<br /><br />And I really feel the need to speak out to the love story. As someone who grew up in a very strict and regimented environment when it came to relationships, I can empathize with Tris and Tobias. And again, the message is simple and well played. It's not about abstinence. It's not about free love. It's about comfort with the person you're with- about what is right for both of you in that moment. It is about physical love coming out of something much deeper. One of the scenes I will never get out of my head is in Divergent when she sees his chest for the first time. The attraction, the fear, the desperate need to find a balance between the ideals being thrown at them is all so true. And so beautiful. And if this series can teach one person to find that balance and really take in the physical part of a relationship when it is right for them, then it has done its job. The way this relationship begins and the way it progresses is natural and understandable, and it grounds the idea of intimacy being intimate without making it into a set of rules or a command.<br /><br />I also am of the opinion that Roth is more talented than this book suggests. This one was rushed. Fans were clamoring for the ending so she wrote it and gave it to them. Yes, she should have taken her time- really read and understood and grounded her story. She rushed it for the public, a mistake I'm sure she will learn from in the future.<br /><br />The book was still interesting. I still read it in three days. I still was shocked and happy and sad, and the ending truly destroyed me when I first read it. Shock value or not- it was beautiful. And in many ways I feel it was right.<br /><br />If you're a fan of her books, don't leave them open ended. Finish the series and accept Allegiant with the grain of salt it deserves. I still personally look forward to things she will work on in the future.

006202406X|2.0|I've found that my opinion of this book has gone down with time, so I'm editing this review....<br /><br />Summary: \\"Allegiant\\" wasn't entirely bad, but it was by far the weakest of the \\"Divergent\\" series, including the short stories.  There were parts that I liked, and at times, it had some of the same \\"I can't stop reading\\" feel that the other books had, but overall it felt very disconnected from the first two books.  The characterizations and voices were different, and the plot had virtually no overlap with the earlier books.  In many ways, the book felt like it had been written by a different author, enough so that at one point I went online to double-check that I had received the right book.<br /><br />On top of that, the book left me with a tremendous feeling of loss that left me unwilling to ever read it again.  Originally, I thought I would get over that and reread at least some parts of it, but I never did.  Instead, my uncertainty turned to dislike, and I eventually removed the book from my Kindle so I wouldn't have to see it when browsing my library.  Even worse, the book also turned me off the first two books in the series for a while.  I don't think I would have gotten over that if it weren't for fanfiction, but fortunately, that restored the rest of the series for me.  Still, I would have to say that on balance, I wish I had never read \\"Allegiant.\\"  It just didn't give me enough to make up for what it took away.<br /><br />At this point, my honest recommendation is to read \\"Divergent\\" and \\"Insurgent\\" and then skip to fanfiction.  There are several alternate third books out there that will give you closure on the series, including one I wrote called \\"Determinant\\" (if you're interested in trying it, search on \\"Windchimed Determinant\\").  There are also hundreds of alternate endings that assume the first part of \\"Allegiant\\" occurred but then pick up partway through that book and provide a happier ending.  If you've already read \\"Allegiant\\" and want to forget it, those endings will help you do that.<br /><br />For those of you who are still undecided, here's a summary of what works and what doesn't in \\"Allegiant\\"....<br /><br />What works:<br /><br />1. You learn more about Tris' mother.  It's a little strange and contradicts the earlier books, but it's there.<br /><br />2. You find out what happened to some characters who were mentioned earlier but not shown.<br /><br />3. You get the story on the world around Chicago, why they're there, and how they came to be.  Unfortunately, it's dull and contradicts the earlier books, but it does offer a type of closure.<br /><br />4. There are some decent scenes.<br /><br />5. It explores concepts you don't often see in YA books -- remorse, change, death, healing, and everyday forms of courage.  In particular, though the end is crushingly sad, you realize that some of the characters improved each others' lives permanently and changed the world around them for the better, and that this effect doesn't go away when they die.  Basically, their lives and relationships were worthwhile, even if much too short.<br /><br />What doesn't work:<br /><br />1. The book lacks emotion.  \\"Divergent\\" and \\"Insurgent\\" have a lot of feeling, both stated and implied.  There are many scenes in those books that I re-read repeatedly, just savoring the feel of them.  This book has very, very little of that, and what it does have isn't as good.  The only feeling that's deep is grief, which is overwhelming at the end, but honestly, most of that grief is because you love the characters from how they were in the earlier books, not for what you saw in this book.<br /><br />2. The narration never finds its groove and doesn't sound like it did in the earlier stories.  Tris's sentence structure is suddenly short, with no \\"poetry\\" to the words, and Tobias/Four doesn't sound at all like he did in the short stories.  Also, they sound so much like each other that I kept forgetting whose POV I was reading.<br /><br />3. Most of the other characters disappear or die without any further development.  They have very few lines, and some of them (including some important ones) literally die without ever speaking a new word.<br /><br />4. The whole faction concept, with all of its interesting elements, basically just goes away.<br /><br />5. There's startlingly little consistency with the earlier books.  Personally, I thought there were subtle clues in the first two books about the characters and about what plot developments were coming, and I expected to see Roth pick up on those the way JK Rowling always did, but absolutely none of them came back that way.  As an example, Tori got through Jeanine's defenses, implying she was Divergent, yet we saw her kill an Abnegation leader during the simulation, implying she wasn't.  I thought we'd learn more about her, but there's nothing.  I expected some big reveals about Caleb and Peter, but those didn't happen either.  Uriah, one of the more interesting side characters, says almost nothing.  The same goes with Evelyn, and the relationship between her and Natalie Prior, and Marcus, and really all of them -- those clues are all left as loose strings.<br /><br />6. The plot is simplistic, inconsistent with the earlier books, and full of holes.  The earlier books had a lot of action and unexpected twists and turns.  This one doesn't.<br /><br />* Warning: The rest of this review contains major spoilers. *<br /><br />To me, the biggest disappointment with Tris wasn't her death.  That was actually the most in-character part of the book.  What frustrated me was how she got there.  She found out that people were going to wipe memories, and she immediately planned to wipe their memories instead, without even trying to think of any other solutions.  Why didn't she offer to go into the city and fix things another way to avoid the need for the reset, the way her mother did years earlier?  Why didn't she at least try to use her influence on David to gethim to see another perspective?  For that matter, she could have kidnapped David and taken him into the city to keep the rest of the Bureau from attacking, or she could have used the memory wipe serum directly on him to get him to forget about the plan.<br /><br />Instead, she jumped straight to the idea of setting off the serum en masse.  And then, after she decided that, she apparently could only think of one way to get it.  She didn't try to figure out the passcode, even though Tobias is great with computers and even though David was obsessed with her mother and might have used her name as a passcode.  She didn't consider attacking the group that was taking the serum to the plane to try to release the serum by shooting the container it was in.  She didn't seem to make any effort at all.  Instead, she just stated that it was a suicide mission and let her brother agree to die for it.  It was so glaringly out of character that I assumed Roth was going to have a bunch of other things happen to change this course of action, but she didn't.<br /><br />Also, I can't help but feel like this entire book was an attack on Tobias.  He had almost nothing good in his very dark life, and this book took away what little he had.  He lost his Divergence, and he lost Tris, and for a while he lost Zeke's friendship.  I suppose the point was to show that life is still worth living no matter what, but it really felt like heaping even more punishment on top of someone who didn't deserve it.  There was no sense of justice to it at all, and that's a horrible feeling to walk away with at the end of a trilogy.  I think that's the biggest reason this book \\"sat\\" so poorly for me.  Injustice always does.

1401235425|5.0|I hate the whole concept of The New 52 universe; an across the board reboot of DC continuity that has all the trappings of a profit grab.  Even someone like me...a primarily Marvel guy with an above average knowledge of most of the DCU...someone who would benefit greatly from such a reboot, I was less than impressed. There were some gems (Teen Titans, Birds of Prey) but if I had to pick the crown jewel it would this title.  While Scott Snyder's dialogue is not quite on par with many of his peers, his plotting, pace, and action scenes employed in this title are some of the best in comics today.  When paired with Greg Capullo's McFarlane-esque visuals the complete package is hard to put down.<br /><br />While many of The New 52 launches kept plots that were fairly close to the origins, Batman's reboot strikes out for bolder ground and for that I applaud it.  Since I've been reading Batman Gotham City has been made into a character all its own.  Batman's love for it and interaction with it has been the centerpiece of many a Batman tale.  Snyder asks the simple question...what if Gotham City had secrets of her own? What if within its shadows is something far more terrifying, far more powerful, than Batman or the many costumed villains he faces? The Batman of this story is every bit a detective as he is a warrior. While this may sound like standard fare, in the hands of Snyder it is something completely different. B00DXP7L88|1.0|***SPOILERS***<br /><br />Incredibly disappointing. I have followed Dexter from Season 1, and have largely been a huge fan of the show. The writing, particularly in the early seasons, was incredible. It was well-executed, highly psychological, and exciting. The show, simply speaking, was 'on', firing on all cylinders. While many have perceived the later seasons as reflecting a decline in terms of direction, writing, excitement, etc., I maintained my appreciation of the show as a whole for many reasons. I also appreciated the development of the characters, the plot lines, and what could have been presented as a satisfying ending to a great show. While I didn't necessarily agree with various choices made (elements of Seasons 5 and 6 come to mind), I understood and accepted the underlying thinking. Season 8, in essence, ran the series into the ground.<br /><br />The writing, overall, for the end of the series comes across as though it was written on a whim, lacking in both thought and direction. This is particularly confusing and curious, considering the essence of the end of the show (in terms of plot) was decided upon as early as two to three years ago, which would seem to suggest that they had more than enough time to plan it out. The show, in the end, felt tired, unimaginative, and illogical. There were many, many gaping plot holes - too many to list. There was considerable oversight. It was not as sharp as it once was - a failure for a show that seemed to pride itself, rightfully so, on its exacting intricacy.<br /><br />The writers pass through a series of personal voyages for all characters that, for the most part, show considerable promise early on. Despite some interesting directions (with Masuka, for instance), in the end, these storylines seem to serve as nothing more than distractions and fillers dangled in front of the viewer. They're dropped, with the excuse given by producers that the show, in the end, centered on Dexter. This, perhaps, would have been acceptable if the show closed Dexter's character in any kind of fitting manner.<br /><br />The finale is exhaustingly illogical and incoherent. Ultimately, it comes across as a closing to a series (or multitude) of disparate plot lines the writers and producers didn't have the ability to effectively manage; they opened up too many doors for which they had neither the ability nor the apparent desire to close appropriately. Worse than this, the writing in the finale is tedious and, some would say, insulting. It is contradictory, self-confirming, and is antithetical to much of the work that was done in building and developing Dexter and Deb as characters. In other words, it seemed like a moral bait-and-switch. The writers towed a moral line through much of the close of the series, examining Dexter's ability (or relative inability) to develop as a person, exhibit and experience humanity and humility, approach some type of normalcy with regard to morality, etc. The end did nothing to bring this to a reasonable  and fitting close. If we are to reasonably believe that the imposition of a type of self-inflicted prison is the most feasible way of giving Dexter what he 'deserves', the writing did very little to support or provide a foundation for that over the last season. The degrading of Deb's character can be couched in terms of analogy to the development of Dexter's. Both are products (victims?) of circumstances. Both deal with the cards that they're dealt with - with sincere and, I would say, moral intentions. Admittedly, attributing fault and following the trail of moral cause and influence of Dexter's and Deb's mutual destruction seems a difficult task; however, the writers didn't even begin to do the topic justice. The notion that Deb is to come to the end that she did and that Dexter is to feel the full weight of it all in the way that he did is laughable. It lacks in any type of moral consistency.<br /><br />It isn't that I have an issue with the decision to kill Deb. I personally would never have conceived of such an ending as being fitting, but I don't base my opinions on that decision. Rather, I base my disappointment on the way in which it was executed - poorly. The treatment of Deb's end (and the show's end) was superficial and absent-minded. The writing, to this end, was also superficial. Any thought or symbolism behind this writing is imagined. Calling a spur-of-the-moment hurricane 'Laura' (Dexter's mother) to present Dexter's origins as a basis for where and how he 'needed' to end; Deb wrapped in white, pure and 'buried' by her brother; Dexter imprisoning himself in Oregon (Dexter, Oregon perhaps?) to impose 'deserved' punishment on himself for his 'destruction' of Deb; etc., etc. - these present the illusion of thought, but are considerably below-the-mark compared with the writing of earlier seasons. Worse, they lack in cohesion.<br /><br />The handling of Dexter's end could not have been more baseless and weak. The potential existed for real strength in what could have been done with Dexter. Conceivably, either killing him off or, conversely, developing him to the point of a 'reformed' (albeit in Dexter's own way), morally-operating individual would have been orders of magnitude more compelling than what was given. What was given was a cop-out - something that allowed the show to drag on without an actual ending and without certainty. Something that still continues to allow the show to drag on aimlessly and mindlessly if you're wont to believe spin-off rumors. In short, the writers had perhaps two choices to make - option one and option two - in dealing with Dexter as a character; two choices that could have been very strong. They chose option three - a lazy and uninspired choice.<br /><br />The producers have since attempted to explain (and defend) their decisions, couching their choices in terms of what they felt was most fitting in ensuring that Dexter felt the full weight of his actions. This, simply speaking, isnonsense. Anyone who claims such writing and direction as having thought, execution, and any type of cohesion is deluded. Anyone who claims that the result of Dexter's character was the only necessary option to ensure that he 'got what he deserved' is also deluded. This is, purely and simply, lazy and unintelligent writing - a vague attempt at what was thought as deep, reflective, and morally-conscious writing. An attempt to finish what was created by those with considerably better ability to manage such a show.<br /><br />Overall, the end lacked considerably in what made Dexter such an engaging show early on. The writing seemed tired, incoherent, and illogical. The direction (or lack thereof) of the final season ran what had considerable promise as a stellar ending to a great series into the ground. Instead, the finale flirted with morality in a way that was absurd, explored erroneous plot lines that insulted viewers' intelligence, and gave an ending that ran counter to what had been developed for the show. A large failure.

006202406X|1.0|Contains spoilers!!<br />I'm so sorry that this series ended the way it did. Unlike many reviewers I don't think this was a rushed decision on Mrs. Roth's part, she states in many interviews she knew how she wanted it to end from the very beginning. I also don't think she did it to be purposely cruel. I think she sees the ending as expressing her personal beliefs as a Christian very well. I also think she seems like a very sweet woman and just because of that I wish I could write a more forgiving review.<br /><br />Instead, having read the book while my daughter did the same, all I can express is utter disappointment. Within the first two books I really saw the character of Tris as a flawed but overall excellent role model, certainly leaps and bounds better than Bella would ever be. I loved that my daughter so connected to this strong and brave character. I actually especially loved that self sacrifice is explored as something that is not always ideal.<br /><br />Looking at this series from a secular feminist perspective I loved that in books 1 and 2 here was an example showing girls that sacrificing all of yourself is not usually the best/right answer. It's pretty much the exact opposite to Bella of Twilight who sacrifices her life for her boyfriend and has rightly been condemned as a weak character. Women in our society constantly hear messages of how we must sacrifice and put ourselves last, to our own detriment since the same is not asked of men.  Finally we had a character and book dealing with this issue in a positive way, all the way until her death. By having her death be her sacrificing herself for her brother everything was basically undone and all character growth was removed. Yes, Mrs. Roth tried to make it all about motivation but the end result was the same, Tris was the same as before she left Abnegation. Had Caleb actually died and willingly sacrificed himself that would have at least shown some character growth for him, though not the level of growth that Tris had experienced. Even had events been the same but Tris had lived the story would have had a much better feel since Tris did believe she had a chance against the Death Serum and was not just throwing her life away originally.  Tris's death truly ruined the end of the series and my one hope is that it is re-written in the movie adaptation. As a parent I find it important for YA novels to end with a sense of hope. Even in The Fault in Our Stars with one character dead and the knowledge that the other is living on borrowed time there was a feeling of hope and meaning. I think Mrs. Roth went for that ending but is to much of a young and inexperienced writer to achieve it yet.<br /><br />I'd like to say I will support Mrs. Roth's future endeavors but right now that seems unlikely. Unless I hear some amazing reviews of her future work we will be staying away.
